<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41598"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Portuguese wild grapevine genome re-sequencing ( Vitis vinifera sylvestris )"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Scientific Reports 2020 10:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-11-04"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="The first genome of Vitis vinifera vinifera (PN40024), published in 2007, boosted grapevine related studies. While this reference genome is a suitable tool for the overall studies in the field, it lacks the ability to unveil changes accumulated during V. v. vinifera domestication. The subspecies V. v. sylvestris preserves wild characteristics, making it a good material to provide insights into V. v. vinifera domestication. The difference in the reproductive strategy between both subspecies is one of the characteristics that set them apart. While V. v. vinifera flowers are hermaphrodite, V. v. sylvestris is mostly dioecious. In this paper, we compare the re-sequencing of the genomes from a male and a female individual of the wild sylvestris, against the reference vinifera genome (PN40024). Variant analysis reveals a low number but with high impact modifications in coding regions, essentially non-synonymous single nucleotide polymorphisms and frame shifts caused by insertions and deletions. The sex-locus was manually inspected, and the results obtained are in line with the most recent works related with wild grapevine sex. In this paper we also describe for the first time RNA editing in transcripts of 14 genes in the sex-determining region, including VviYABBY and VviPLATZ."/>

                                </li>
                            
                        </ul>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>The first genome of <i>Vitis vinifera vinifera</i> (PN40024), published in 2007, boosted grapevine related studies. While this reference genome is a suitable tool for the overall studies in the field, it lacks the ability to unveil changes accumulated during <i>V. v. vinifera</i> domestication. The subspecies <i>V. v. sylvestris</i> preserves wild characteristics, making it a good material to provide insights into <i>V. v. vinifera</i> domestication. The difference in the reproductive strategy between both subspecies is one of the characteristics that set them apart. While <i>V. v. vinifera</i> flowers are hermaphrodite, <i>V. v. sylvestris</i> is mostly dioecious. In this paper, we compare the re-sequencing of the genomes from a male and a female individual of the wild <i>sylvestris</i>, against the reference <i>vinifera</i> genome (PN40024). Variant analysis reveals a low number but with high impact modifications in coding regions, essentially non-synonymous single nucleotide polymorphisms and frame shifts caused by insertions and deletions. The sex-locus was manually inspected, and the results obtained are in line with the most recent works related with wild grapevine sex. In this paper we also describe for the first time RNA editing in transcripts of 14 genes in the sex-determining region, including <i>VviYABBY</i> and <i>VviPLATZ</i>.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

        </a>
    </div>
    

                        
                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p><i>Vitis vinifera vinifera</i> (hereafter <i>vinifera</i>) is a worldwide important crop, mainly due to wine production. The first <i>Vinifera</i> genomes versions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d342e481">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Vitulo, N. et al. A deep survey of alternative splicing in grape reveals changes in the splicing machinery related to tissue, stress condition and genotype. BMC Plant Biol. 14, 99 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d342e484">2</a></sup>, were obtained from PN40024 genotype, a Pinot Noir<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d342e488">1</a></sup> variety back crossed to reach 93% homozygosity, back in 2005. In the recent past, the <i>Vitis</i> genus has received more attention and the sequences of more varieties were made available<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chin, C.-S. et al. Phased diploid genome assembly with single-molecule real-time sequencing. Nat. Methods 13, 1050–1054 (2016)." href="#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d342e496">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Minio, A., Lin, J., Gaut, B. S. &amp; Cantu, D. How single molecule real-time sequencing and haplotype phasing have enabled reference-grade diploid genome assembly of wine grapes. Front. Plant Sci. 8, 826 (2017)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d342e496_1">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Minio, A. et al. Iso-Seq allows genome-independent transcriptome profiling of grape berry development. G3 Genes Genomes Genet. 9, 755–767 (2019)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d342e496_2">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Minio, A., Massonnet, M., Figueroa-Balderas, R., Castro, A. &amp; Cantu, D. Diploid genome assembly of the wine grape Carménère. G3 Genes Genomes Genet. 9, 1331–1337 (2019)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d342e496_3">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Vondras, A. M. et al. The genomic diversification of grapevine clones. BMC Genom. 20, 972 (2019)." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d342e496_4">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Liang, Z. et al. Whole-genome resequencing of 472 Vitis accessions for grapevine diversity and demographic history analyses. Nat. Commun. 10, 1190 (2019)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d342e496_5">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhou, Y. et al. The population genetics of structural variants in grapevine domestication. Nat. Plants 5, 965–979 (2019)." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d342e496_6">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Mercenaro, L., Nieddu, G., Porceddu, A., Pezzotti, M. &amp; Camiolo, S. Sequence polymorphisms and structural variations among four grapevine (Vitis vinifera L.) cultivars representing sardinian agriculture. Front. Plant Sci. 8, 1279 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d342e499">10</a></sup>. Although the contribution of these <i>vinifera</i> genomes is out of question for the present knowledge, they are limited in providing information on particular changes that occurred during domestication. One of the most relevant subspecies of <i>V. vinifera</i> history is <i>Vitis vinifera sylvestris</i> (hereafter <i>sylvestris</i>), which some authors have referred as <i>vinifera</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Zhou, Y., Massonnet, M., Sanjak, J., Cantu, D. &amp; Gaut, B. S. The evolutionary genomics of grape (Vitis vinifera ssp. vinifera) domestication. PNAS 114, 11715–11720 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d342e518">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="McGovern, P. E. Ancient Wine: The Search for the Origins of Viniculture (Princeton University Press, Princeton, 2004)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d342e521">12</a></sup>. <i>Sylvestris</i>, free from human selection, may provide clues to explain <i>vinifera</i> domestication. This human intervention was an economically important step for grapevine, responsible for morphological changes that include larger berry and bunch size, higher sugar content, altered seed morphology, and a shift from dioecy to a hermaphroditic mating system<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="This, P., Lacombe, T., Cadle-Davidson, M. &amp; Owens, C. L. Wine grape (Vitis vinifera L.) color associates with allelic variation in the domestication gene VvmybA1. Theor. Appl. Genet. 114, 723–730 (2007)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d342e531">13</a></sup>. The dioecious <i>sylvestris</i>, produce female individuals with reflexed stamens, where infertile pollen is produced, whereas male individuals are unable to produce grapes as flowers lacks a functional carpel<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Caporali, E., Spada, A., Marziani, G., Failla, O. &amp; Scienza, A. The arrest of development of abortive reproductive organs in the unisexual flower of Vitis vinifera ssp. silvestris. Sex. Plant Reprod. 15, 291–300 (2003)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d342e538">14</a></sup>.</p><p>In the <i>Vitis</i> genus, several studies have contributed to partially uncover the genomic regions that may be responsible for the differences in flower development between hermaphrodites and dioecious plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Picq, S. et al. A small XY chromosomal region explains sex determination in wild dioecious V. vinifera and the reversal to hermaphroditism in domesticated grapevines. BMC Plant Biol. 14, 229 (2014)." href="#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d342e548">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Fechter, I. et al. Candidate genes within a 143 kb region of the flower sex locus in Vitis. Mol. Genet. Genom. 287, 247–259 (2012)." href="#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d342e548_1">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Coito, J. L. et al. VviAPRT3 and VviFSEX: two genes involved in sex specification able to distinguish different flower types in Vitis. Front. Plant Sci. 8, 98 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d342e551">17</a></sup>. However, the identified regions (chr2:4,907,434.0.5,050,616)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Picq, S. et al. A small XY chromosomal region explains sex determination in wild dioecious V. vinifera and the reversal to hermaphroditism in domesticated grapevines. BMC Plant Biol. 14, 229 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d342e555">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Fechter, I. et al. Candidate genes within a 143 kb region of the flower sex locus in Vitis. Mol. Genet. Genom. 287, 247–259 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d342e558">16</a></sup> are poorly sequenced, assembled and annotated in the canonical reference genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d342e562">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Vitulo, N. et al. A deep survey of alternative splicing in grape reveals changes in the splicing machinery related to tissue, stress condition and genotype. BMC Plant Biol. 14, 99 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d342e565">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Zhou, Y., Massonnet, M., Sanjak, J., Cantu, D. &amp; Gaut, B. S. The evolutionary genomics of grape (Vitis vinifera ssp. vinifera) domestication. PNAS 114, 11715–11720 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d342e568">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="This, P., Lacombe, T., Cadle-Davidson, M. &amp; Owens, C. L. Wine grape (Vitis vinifera L.) color associates with allelic variation in the domestication gene VvmybA1. Theor. Appl. Genet. 114, 723–730 (2007)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d342e571">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Coito, J. L. et al. VviAPRT3 and VviFSEX: two genes involved in sex specification able to distinguish different flower types in Vitis. Front. Plant Sci. 8, 98 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d342e574">17</a></sup>. Recently, two new studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Badouin, H. et al. The wild grape genome sequence provides insights into the transition from dioecy to hermaphroditism during grape domestication. bioRxiv 2020.01.07.897082 (2020). &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1101/2020.01.07.897082&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d342e578">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Massonnet, M. et al. The genetic basis of sex determination in grapes. Nat. Commun. 11, 1–12 (2020)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d342e581">19</a></sup> suggested the possible players involved in <i>sylvestris</i> sex specification by uncovering <i>INAPERTURATE POLLEN1</i> (<i>INP1</i>) as a male sterility gene and proposing a set of genes responsible for female sterility.</p><p>The understanding of all players involved in <i>Vitis vinifera sylvestris</i> dioecy is still incomplete. Events as RNA editing in nuclear transcripts have already been proven in various organisms, as humans<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Xu, G. &amp; Zhang, J. Human coding RNA editing is generally nonadaptive. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. 111, 3769–3774 (2014)." href="#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d342e601">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chen, L. Characterization and comparison of human nuclear and cytosolic editomes. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. 110, E2741–E2747 (2013)." href="#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d342e601_1">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Wang, I. X. et al. ADAR regulates RNA editing, transcript stability, and gene expression. Cell Rep. 5, 849–860 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d342e604">22</a></sup>, cephalopods<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Liscovitch-Brauer, N. et al. Trade-off between transcriptome plasticity and genome evolution in cephalopods. Cell 169, 191-202.e11 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d342e608">23</a></sup>, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Qulsum, U., Azad, Md. T. A. &amp; Tsukahara, T. Analysis of tissue-specific RNA editing events of genes involved in RNA editing in Arabidopsis thaliana. J. Plant Biol. 62, 351–358 (2019)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d342e614">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Meng, Y. et al. RNA editing of nuclear transcripts in Arabidopsis thaliana. BMC Genom. 11, S12 (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d342e617">25</a></sup> and <i>Vitis vinifera sylvestris</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Ramos, M. J. N. et al. RNA editing in inflorescences of wild grapevine unveils association to sex and development. bioRxiv 2020.03.03.974626 (2020). &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1101/2020.03.03.974626&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d342e624">26</a></sup>.</p><p>RNA editing, defined as post-transcriptional alterations of RNA molecules by insertion, deletion or modification, not including processes as splicing, capping or polyadenylation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Xu, G. &amp; Zhang, J. Human coding RNA editing is generally nonadaptive. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. 111, 3769–3774 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d342e631">20</a></sup>, may also contribute to sex differentiation in <i>V. v. sylvestris</i>.</p><p>The aim of this paper is to compare the genomes of <i>sylvestris</i> against PN40024 and establish the differences that can highlight the main domestication events. To achieved the proposed aim, we defined four major tasks: (1) to characterize the re-sequencing of <i>sylvestris</i>, considering two distinct individuals, one with male flowers and a second one with female flowers; (2) to compare in detail the genomes of the <i>sylvestris</i> individuals to the reference genome, in terms of Single Nucleotide Polymorphisms (SNPs) and Insertions/Deletions (InDels); (3) to compare the different alleles found for each <i>sylvestris</i> specimen, focusing on chromosome 2, where the sex-determining region has been proposed to be present; (4) to access RNA editing events in genes associated to the sex-determining region.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><p>The genomes of two wild-type individuals (<i>sylvestris</i>) were obtained by Illumina (2 × 125) and the reads were mapped against the reference genome of <i>vinifera</i> PN40024<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d342e668">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Vitulo, N. et al. A deep survey of alternative splicing in grape reveals changes in the splicing machinery related to tissue, stress condition and genotype. BMC Plant Biol. 14, 99 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d342e671">2</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#Fig1">1</a>). The reference genome used in this study was firstly published in 2007 after controlled breeding of Pinot Noir, creating a lineage with about 93% homozygosity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d342e678">1</a></sup>. This genome (8X coverage) consists in 487 Mb spread into 3,514 scaffolds (N<sub>50</sub> = 2.07 Mb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d342e685">1</a></sup>. Scaffolds were further associated within the 19 chromosomes of <i>Vitis</i> plus 13 virtual chromosomes, corresponding to 12 random chromosomes (associated to the real chromosomes 1, 3, 4, 5, 7, 9, 11, 12, 13, 16, 17 and 18) and a completely unknown chromosome, where sequences that were not fully associated with a real chromosome were deposited<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d342e692">1</a></sup>. The sequenced genome was further improved with a 12X coverage and subsequent gene annotations were published<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d342e696">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Vitulo, N. et al. A deep survey of alternative splicing in grape reveals changes in the splicing machinery related to tissue, stress condition and genotype. BMC Plant Biol. 14, 99 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d342e699">2</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Figure 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-020-76012-6/MediaObjects/41598_2020_76012_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-020-76012-6/MediaObjects/41598_2020_76012_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1272" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Sampling material of <i>Vitis vinifera sylvestris</i> (wild type) and flowchart of data analysis steps. WM individual show carpel abortion (typical of a male flower type); WF individual has reflexed stamens (representing a female flower type). Reference genome is PN40024 (CRIBI, 12X).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">The majority of the wild-type reads map to the reference genome</h3><p>Around 97% of the high-quality reads obtained were successfully mapped to the reference genome: 478,309,356 reads in total (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#Tab1">1</a>). After refinement steps (please see Materials and Methods for details), 92% of the mapped reads were kept. From those, 89% mapped only once to the reference genome (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#Tab1">1</a>). About 10% of the mapped reads lost their pair in the process or mapped onto a different chromosome (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#Tab1">1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Number of wild-type reads mapped against the reference genome (PN40024).</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Comparison between the wild-type and reference genomes</h3><p>Single Nucleotide Polymorphism (SNP) and Insertions/Deletions (InDels) analysis were performed between the sequences obtained for the two wild-type individuals and the reference genome. Wild-type individuals used on this re-sequencing project present distinct flower characteristics. One of the plants show male characteristics (WM), with no functional carpel and the other exhibits female flowers (WF) with a complete carpel, but with reflexed stamens producing infertile pollen. Both of these individuals had almost the same number of SNPs identified (6,197,145 for WF and 6,575,026 for WM individual), and shared half of the occurrences (3,643,713 SNPs; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#Fig2">2</a>a). The InDels analysis showed the same tendency as the previous results: the number of occurrences in both individuals were similar (1,151,997 for WF and 1,177,918 events for WM) and about half of them were shared (637,320 events; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#Fig2">2</a>b).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Figure 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-020-76012-6/MediaObjects/41598_2020_76012_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-020-76012-6/MediaObjects/41598_2020_76012_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="628" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Landscape of SNPs and InDels in the two wild-type genomes. (<b>a</b>) Venn diagrams showing the number of SNPs. (<b>b</b>) Venn diagrams showing the number of InDels. Comparison was performed against the reference genome (PN40024, CRIBI, 12X). (<b>c</b>) Transition over transversion ratio (Ti/Tv) of WM (male) and WF (female) individuals according to the genomic region. For exomes, analysis was split into synonymous and non-synonymous SNPs.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The ratio between the numbers of transitions over transversions (Ti/Tv) is usually calculated in order to assess the quality of an assembly or SNPs calling. As Ti/Tv ration has been reported dependent of the genomic context of the SNPs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Wang, J., Raskin, L., Samuels, D. C., Shyr, Y. &amp; Guo, Y. Genome measures used for quality control are dependent on gene function and ancestry. Bioinformatics 31, 318–323 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d342e981">27</a></sup>, we have evaluated Ti/Tv on introns (ratio 2.1), exons with synonymous SNP (1.9), exons with non-synonymous SNPs (1.4) and intergenic regions (2.2) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#Fig2">2</a>c). Our analysis confirmed that Ti/Tv was dependent of the genomic location, but not on the individuals under study, since WM and WF presented the same Ti/Tv pattern (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#Fig2">2</a>c).</p><p>The distribution of SNPs and InDels, on the genome, was evaluated for each chromosome, by calculating the frequency of occurrences (number of SNPs/InDels by chromosome size). In both SNPs and InDels, the chromosome 6 was the one with the lowest number of events. For this chromosome, 1.00% of the bases were found to be changes on a single nucleotide (frequency of 0.011; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#Fig3">3</a>a and Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#MOESM1">S1</a>) and InDels were identified in 0.20% of the chromosome (frequency of 0.0020; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#Fig3">3</a>b and Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#MOESM1">S1</a>). The low number of modifications in chromosome 6 is essentially caused by WF individual, which presents a fraction of SNPs below Tukey ‘s fence. The least conserved chromosome was the 9 with a percentage of SNPs of 1.5% (frequency 0.015; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#Fig3">3</a>a and Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#MOESM1">S1</a>) and InDels covering 0.29% of this chromosome size (0.0029; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#Fig3">3</a>b and Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#MOESM1">S1</a>). The modification enrichment in chromosome 9 is mainly due to the WM individual. The pairwise comparison between WM and WF, besides chromosome 6 distinct profiles between the two tested individuals, as referred, also highlights the higher number of modifications in WM chromosome 13 than in WF (Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#MOESM1">S2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Figure 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-020-76012-6/MediaObjects/41598_2020_76012_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-020-76012-6/MediaObjects/41598_2020_76012_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1103" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Analysis SNPs distribution and dynamic. (<b>a</b>) Frequency of events per chromosome length (base pairs) in considering SNPs and (<b>b</b>) InDels. Red and blue lines represent the Tukey's outlier fences (k = 1.5). (<b>c</b>) Number of transversions (A ↔ C, A ↔ T, C ↔ G and G ↔ T) and transitions (A ↔ G and C ↔ T). Reference: The nucleotide in the reference genome (PN40024, CRIBI, 12X); <i>V. v. sylvestris</i>: the nucleotides identified in WM and WF genomes.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The analysis of chromosome regions with higher and fewer number of modifications, carried out in both individuals, showed that the density varied along the chromosomes, however most of them do not have a specific location with high density of SNPs and InDels simultaneously (Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#MOESM1">S3</a>).</p><p>We analysed the nature of the substitutions in SNPs by comparing the nucleotides on the reference genome with the nucleotides observed for the wild type. In accordance with other studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Mercenaro, L., Nieddu, G., Porceddu, A., Pezzotti, M. &amp; Camiolo, S. Sequence polymorphisms and structural variations among four grapevine (Vitis vinifera L.) cultivars representing sardinian agriculture. Front. Plant Sci. 8, 1279 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d342e1062">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="McGovern, P. E. Ancient Wine: The Search for the Origins of Viniculture (Princeton University Press, Princeton, 2004)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d342e1065">12</a></sup>, the number of transitions (substitution between adenosine and guanine or cytosine and thymine) were much more frequent on both genomes than transversions (substitutions between adenosine and cytosine, adenosine and thymine, cytosine and guanine or guanine and thymine) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#Fig3">3</a>c and Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#MOESM1">S4</a>).</p><p>The effects of SNPs on protein-coding regions were also investigated by analysing the SNP location within gene context and the impact caused. To perform this analysis, we have calculated the percentage of modified features. As expected, the most affected context by SNPs was the intergenic (about 4.1% of intergenic sites reveal differences compared to reference genome; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#Fig4">4</a>a and Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#MOESM1">S5</a>). Upstream, UTR regions and downstream are also affected regions (ranging from 1.3 to 0.9%). Introns and CDS regions reveal almost the same levels of SNPs, in terms of percentages (0.6% and 0.7%, respectively). When SNPs affected CDS, we further studied the in silico impact expected (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#Fig4">4</a>b and Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#MOESM1">S5</a>). The percentage of CDS modifications with synonymous and non-synonymous impact was almost the same (0.3% and 0.4%, respectively). A high percentage of premature STOP codons was found, essentially considering that this impact is among the most disruptive ones (0.01%).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Figure 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-020-76012-6/MediaObjects/41598_2020_76012_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-020-76012-6/MediaObjects/41598_2020_76012_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="928" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>The gene context of the identified modification in wild-type genomes (WM and WF). (<b>a</b>) Percentage of regions affected by SNPs and (<b>b</b>) the impact caused by the SNP when it occurs in CDS regions. (<b>c</b>) Percentage of features affected by InDels and (<b>d</b>) its impact in CDS regions. Reference genome and annotation: PN40024, CRIBI, 12X V2.1. For events between genes, the most relevant context was chosen (e.g. upstream instead of downstream), as described by SnpEff documentation. Upstream and downstream were considered for a 5 kbp range.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The overall distribution of InDels is almost identical to SNPs: the majority of indels occurs in intergenic (0.6%), followed by upstream, UTRs and downstream regions (ranging between 0.3% and 0.2%; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#Fig4">4</a>c and Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#MOESM1">S5</a>). However, contrary to SNPs, only a small portion of CDS are affected by InDels (0.03%). In terms of impact (considering CDS context), the majority of InDels caused frame shift (0.02%; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#Fig4">4</a>d and Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#MOESM1">S5</a>). InDels resulting in codon change + deletions, corresponding to events where at least one codon is changed and at least one is deleted (0.003%), codon insertion (0.002%), codon change with insertions and simple codon deletion (both lower than 0.002%) were also found (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#Fig4">4</a>d and Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#MOESM1">S5</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Heterozygosity levels</h3><p>The nature of the SNPs and InDels was evaluated regarding their homozygosity. Most of the events were heterozygous (0/1 and 1/2) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#Fig5">5</a> and Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#MOESM1">S6</a>). The <i>sylvestris</i> individuals had about 4,104,719 heterozygous SNPs and 761,638 heterozygous InDels, being the WM plant more heterozygous than the WF (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#Fig5">5</a>). The number of homozygous alterations between <i>sylvestris</i> and the reference genome (1/1) was much lower (2,281,366 homozygous SNPs and 403,321 homozygous InDels) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#Fig5">5</a> and Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#MOESM1">S6</a>). Combining SNPs and InDels, the total number of heterozygous <i>loci</i> (0/1 and 1/2) was 4,866,357 while the homozygous (1/1) changes were 631,487. The ratio between heterozygous/homozygous SNPs was 1.80 and1.89 for InDels.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Figure 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-020-76012-6/MediaObjects/41598_2020_76012_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-020-76012-6/MediaObjects/41598_2020_76012_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="954" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>General heterozygosity analysis. Number of homozygous (1/1) and heterozygous (0/1 and 1/2) <i>loci</i> evaluated for the wild type re-sequenced genomes. (<b>a</b>) Evaluation according to SNPs. (<b>b</b>) Evaluation according to InDels. (<b>c</b>) Heterozygous over non-reference homozygous ratio (he/nonref-hom) for each individual (WM and WF) according to multiple genomic locations. 0/1 represents <i>loci</i> where two alleles were observed, one being identical to reference genome (PN40024, CRIBI, 12X); 1/1 represent <i>loci</i> where only one allele was observed, being different than reference; 1/2 represents heterozygous <i>loci</i>, being both alleles different than reference.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>An analysis of the heterozygous (0/1 and 1/2) over non-reference homozygous (1/1) SNPs, considering the <i>sylvestris</i> individuals separated, at multiple genomic locations, revealed slightly differences between them (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#Fig5">5</a>c). Ratios obtained were 2.2 for WM (introns and exons), 2.0 for WM (intergenics), 2.0 for WF (exome) and 1.8 for WF (introns and intergenics) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#Fig5">5</a>c). When considering exons, the ratios obtained for synonymous and non-synonymous were the same (data not shown).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Validation of genome sequencing by Sanger analysis</h3><p>Some of the SNPs detected on the presented comparison were validated by Sanger sequencing, targeting modifications in four different genes. From the nine modifications tested, four were homozygous and shared by both individuals, suggesting that these modifications are exclusive of wild-type individuals (Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#MOESM1">S7</a>a, b and c). One of the modifications shared by both individuals represented a case where the wild type genomes are heterozygous (Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#MOESM1">S7</a>d). We also validated the occurrences of individual exclusive modifications, where one of the individuals (WF) was homozygous and the other (WM) was found to be heterozygous (Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#MOESM1">S7</a>d). All the modifications identified in Sanger analysis were in accordance with Illumina results. Although, a higher number of changes were detected than those presented in the Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#MOESM1">S7</a>, the ones presented are an example to validate the results.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">De novo assembly metrics</h3><p>A de novo assembly of the genomes was achieved to better assess the sex-determined region. All the reads obtained in this project were cleaned to remove low confidence reads. The female and male assembly used 224,542,530 and 287,279,296 reads, respectively (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#Tab2">2</a>). The coverage estimates for this assembly was 58X to female and 74X to male individual (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#Tab2">2</a>). After the assembly, the female genome was comprised into 102,650 contigs with a total size of 364,897,899 bp (N50 of 5,795), whereas the male was represented by 108,550 contigs, totalling 370,798,370 bp (N50 of 5,317; Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#Tab2">2</a>). A %CG ratio of 34 was identified for both samples under study.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Parameters related to de novo assembly of the wild female (WF) and male (WM) individuals. Total number of reads: the number of reads obtained from the sequencing. Reads used on the assembly: number of reads after cleaning with BBDuk (BBMap package). Coverage: Expected coverage, considering the reference genome of 486 Mbp. Number of contigs: The amount of contigs obtained from the assemblies with more than 1kbp. Total assembly size: The total size (in base pairs) of the obtained genome. N50: The size of the shortest contig at 50% of the total genome length. %CG: Percentage of Cytosine and Guanines over the total genome length.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Manually inspection of chromosome 2</h3><p>Recently, knowledge about the sex-determining region was updated<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Badouin, H. et al. The wild grape genome sequence provides insights into the transition from dioecy to hermaphroditism during grape domestication. bioRxiv 2020.01.07.897082 (2020). &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1101/2020.01.07.897082&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d342e1445">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Massonnet, M. et al. The genetic basis of sex determination in grapes. Nat. Commun. 11, 1–12 (2020)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d342e1448">19</a></sup>, and the 24 genes located in the sex-determining region were manually inspected from a de novo assembly performed with the raw data obtained for both male (WM) and female (WF) <i>sylvestris</i> individuals. The number of contigs identified for each one of these genes was listed in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#MOESM2">S1</a>. Both studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Badouin, H. et al. The wild grape genome sequence provides insights into the transition from dioecy to hermaphroditism during grape domestication. bioRxiv 2020.01.07.897082 (2020). &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1101/2020.01.07.897082&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d342e1458">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Massonnet, M. et al. The genetic basis of sex determination in grapes. Nat. Commun. 11, 1–12 (2020)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d342e1461">19</a></sup> indicate that the gene coding for <i>INAPERTURATE POLLEN1</i> (<i>VviINP1</i>) is responsible for male sterility, due to an 8-bp deletion in the CDS region, detected in female individuals, causing a premature stop codon. We found that this gene has homology with VIT_200s0233g00051 and VIT_202s0154g00120, associated with chromosomes “unknown” and 2, respectively. We consider that these two IDs are two alleles of the same gene. In the samples tested under this re-sequencing project, we identified the two alleles in the male individual, but the female individual only has the allele with the 8-bp deletion (VIT_202s0154g00120). Although there was a consensus on the identification of <i>VviINP1</i> as responsible for male sterility, the results for a female sterility gene were not yet unanimous and several genes were proposed as responsible for this trait. The most promising gene reported for female sterility were <i>VviYABBY</i> (VIT_202s0154g00070) and <i>VviPLATZ</i> (VIT_202s0154g00150)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Badouin, H. et al. The wild grape genome sequence provides insights into the transition from dioecy to hermaphroditism during grape domestication. bioRxiv 2020.01.07.897082 (2020). &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1101/2020.01.07.897082&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d342e1481">18</a></sup>. As far as we were able to identify, both of these genes only presented one allele in the hermaphrodite reference genome. In our wild plants the genes were heterozygous in the male and homozygous in the female plants.</p><p>We also manually inspected several genes on chromosome 2, some of them described to be involved in flower development like <i>VviNGA1</i> (VIT_202s0025g03000)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Ballester, P., Navarrete-Gómez, M., Carbonero, P., Oñate-Sánchez, L. &amp; Ferrándiz, C. Leaf expansion in Arabidopsis is controlled by a TCP-NGA regulatory module likely conserved in distantly related species. Physiol. Plant 155, 21–32 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d342e1491">30</a></sup> and <i>VviSLK2</i> (VIT_202s0025g04390)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Shrestha, B., Guragain, B. &amp; Sridhar, V. V. Involvement of co-repressor LUH and the adapter proteins SLK1 and SLK2 in the regulation of abiotic stress response genes in Arabidopsis. BMC Plant Biol. 14, 54 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d342e1498">31</a></sup>. Other 20 genes located on chromosome 2, with distinct transcription patterns were previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Picq, S. et al. A small XY chromosomal region explains sex determination in wild dioecious V. vinifera and the reversal to hermaphroditism in domesticated grapevines. BMC Plant Biol. 14, 229 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d342e1502">15</a></sup> detected<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Fechter, I. et al. Candidate genes within a 143 kb region of the flower sex locus in Vitis. Mol. Genet. Genom. 287, 247–259 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d342e1507">16</a></sup> and were subject to this analysis, in a total of 46 genes. From the 46 genes analysed, the male individual studied was heterozygous for 24 genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#Fig6">6</a>). The alleles of three of WM genes were identical to the reference genome (VIT_202s0241g00140, VIT_202s0154g00230, VIT_202s0033g00020; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#Fig6">6</a>). In the female individual, 9 out of 46 genes were heterozygous with both alleles presenting differences compared to the reference genome; only two of 46 genes had alleles identical to the reference genome (VIT_202s0025g02630 and VIT_202s0033g00020); and the remaining 36 genes were homozygous with differences when compared to the reference. Also, we found only one identical gene between male and female individuals (VIT_202s0154g00030) being homozygous, but distinct to the reference genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#Fig6">6</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Figure 6"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 6</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-020-76012-6/MediaObjects/41598_2020_76012_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-020-76012-6/MediaObjects/41598_2020_76012_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="471" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>Representation of location and differences between the most relevant genes and sex-associated <i>loci</i> in chromosome 2 after manual validation. Top panel represents genes in chromosome 2, with putative impact on sexual structures according to literature; bottom panel represents genes in the sex-determining region (chromosome 2). Orange bars represent alleles of the de novo assembled genomes identical to the reference (PN40024, CRIBI, 12X). Green bars represent alleles identified in the male individual. Blue bars indicate distinct alleles observed in the female individual. When a male and a female allele were identical, the same colour was used (green). Question marks refer to genes with low assembly resolution whose sequence was not identified correctly. Stars indicate genes where RNA editing was identified in transcripts, detected by comparing the genome sequences against the previous published transcriptomes, for the same individuals.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">RNA editing was found in transcripts of the sex-determining region</h3><p>RNA editing differences, defined as differences found in the RNA after transcription, including nucleotides substitutions, were identified in <i>Vitis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Ramos, M. J. N. et al. RNA editing in inflorescences of wild grapevine unveils association to sex and development. bioRxiv 2020.03.03.974626 (2020). &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1101/2020.03.03.974626&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d342e1551">26</a></sup>, using these individuals and previous transcriptomic data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Ramos, M. J. N. et al. Flower development and sex specification in wild grapevine. BMC Genom. 15, 1095 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d342e1555">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Ramos, M. J. N. et al. Deep analysis of wild Vitis flower transcriptome reveals unexplored genome regions associated with sex specification. Plant Mol. Biol. 93, 151–170 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d342e1558">33</a></sup>. It is relevant to report here, that no RNA editing was found for <i>VviINP1</i> accessions (VIT_200s0233g00051 and VIT_202s0154g00120). We found RNA editing in the 3′ UTR transcripts of <i>VviYABBY</i> (chr2:4,864,628), in female flower at developmental stage D, where a thymine was replaced by an adenosine in some transcripts (mRNA two-variants) (Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#MOESM1">S8</a>a). In WM (chr2:4,864,509) we identified and validate by Sanger sequencing a mRNA two-variants modification in developmental stages D and H (Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#MOESM1">S9</a>a). We also found RNA editing in <i>VviPLATZ</i> transcripts, where female and male inflorescences show different RNA editing patterns: the female only presents one event (chr2:4,950,043) (Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#MOESM1">S8</a>a), and the male plant has six events (chr2:4,950,184; chr2:4,950,199; chr2:4,950,364; chr2:4,950,393; chr2:4,950,435 and chr2:4,950,443), totalling seven RNA edited <i>loci</i> (Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#MOESM1">S8</a>b). From these seven <i>loci</i>, only two (both in male plants) are not in the coding region (chr2:4,950,184 and chr2:4,950,199) (Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#MOESM1">S8</a>b). The in silico analysis revealed seven genes in which RNA editing (six in SDR and one outside) was found in WM but only <i>VviPLATZ</i> showed a non-synonymous modification. In WF, nine out of twenty-three genes with RNA editing showed non-synonymous modification, which includes <i>VviPLATZ</i> and <i>VviFSEX</i> (VIT_202s0154g00200) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#Fig6">6</a>).</p><p>To confirm that RNA editing is not an exclusive event in these two individuals (WM and WF), we performed a second validation using a distinct female plant (WF_2). This validation targeted VIT_202s0033g01400, a gene in chromosome 2 far from the sex-determining region. We found several editing events, some with the two forms of mRNA, edited and unedited and one full edited event (Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#MOESM1">S9</a>b). The RNA editing tested by the Sanger sequencing performed on the WF_2 individual, revealed the same changes detect by NGS for WF, suggesting that RNA editing is not an exclusive event of WF and WM individuals.</p><p>Modified mRNAs were found inside and outside the sex-determining region, with distinct profiles between male and female individuals (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#Fig6">6</a>).</p><p>The results reported in this paper, provided a comparison between the reference genome (<i>vinifera</i>) and <i>sylvestris</i>. We have identified SNPs and InDels between the two subspecies and investigated the sex <i>locus</i> region, providing a further evidence of which genes may be key players in sex specification of grapevine. We have also found RNA editing in genes belonging to the sex-determined region, namely <i>VviYABBY</i> and <i>VviPLATZ</i>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>The analysis of the wild-type individuals (<i>V. v. sylvestris</i>) reveals a high similarity to PN40024, used as reference genome (<i>V. v. vinifera</i>). In fact, 89% of the sequences maps only once to PN40024 genome. 10% of the reads whose pair was lost or map to a different chromosome may be due to the remaining heterozygosity present in the reference genome (93% homozygous<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d342e1651">1</a></sup>), namely, due to the 13 virtual chromosomes created, where heterozygous sequences may reside<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d342e1655">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Vitulo, N. et al. A deep survey of alternative splicing in grape reveals changes in the splicing machinery related to tissue, stress condition and genotype. BMC Plant Biol. 14, 99 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d342e1658">2</a></sup>, or other repetitive regions as transposable elements or telomeres, among others. As referred above, we have identified an average of 4,104,719 heterozygous SNPs and 761,638 heterozygous InDels. A similar study from 2017<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Mercenaro, L., Nieddu, G., Porceddu, A., Pezzotti, M. &amp; Camiolo, S. Sequence polymorphisms and structural variations among four grapevine (Vitis vinifera L.) cultivars representing sardinian agriculture. Front. Plant Sci. 8, 1279 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d342e1662">10</a></sup> comparing four grapevine cultivars obtained heterozygous SNPs ranging between 178,650 and 934,946 and heterozygous InDels of 16,768–47,852. Discrepant values between cultivars and <i>sylvestris</i> plants, had already been reported considering only deleterious alleles<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Zhou, Y., Massonnet, M., Sanjak, J., Cantu, D. &amp; Gaut, B. S. The evolutionary genomics of grape (Vitis vinifera ssp. vinifera) domestication. PNAS 114, 11715–11720 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d342e1670">11</a></sup>. The higher number obtained in our study suggests a large distance between wild-type individuals (<i>sylvestris</i>) and the domesticated grapevine (<i>vinifera</i>). The ratios heterozygous/homozygous are in the same range as calculated by Mercenaro et al. (2017)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Mercenaro, L., Nieddu, G., Porceddu, A., Pezzotti, M. &amp; Camiolo, S. Sequence polymorphisms and structural variations among four grapevine (Vitis vinifera L.) cultivars representing sardinian agriculture. Front. Plant Sci. 8, 1279 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d342e1680">10</a></sup>, and between the findings reported by Zhou et al. (2017)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Zhou, Y., Massonnet, M., Sanjak, J., Cantu, D. &amp; Gaut, B. S. The evolutionary genomics of grape (Vitis vinifera ssp. vinifera) domestication. PNAS 114, 11715–11720 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d342e1684">11</a></sup> (~ 77%) and D’Onofrio (2020)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="D’Onofrio, C. Introgression among cultivated and wild grapevine in Tuscany. Front. Plant Sci. 11, 202 (2020)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d342e1689">34</a></sup> (~ 40%).</p><p>It is described that domestication pressure set on cultivated varieties, improving grape characteristics, affected all chromosomes in a similar way<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Zhou, Y., Massonnet, M., Sanjak, J., Cantu, D. &amp; Gaut, B. S. The evolutionary genomics of grape (Vitis vinifera ssp. vinifera) domestication. PNAS 114, 11715–11720 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d342e1696">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Zhou, Y., Muyle, A. &amp; Gaut, B. S. Evolutionary genomics and the domestication of grapes. in The grape genome (eds. Cantu, D. &amp; Walker, M. A.) 39–55 (Springer International Publishing, 2019). &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-030-18601-2_3&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d342e1699">35</a></sup>. In the present study, we were unable to detect a particular chromosome with significantly different number of modifications, measured by a single nucleotide change or Insertion/Deletion. Nevertheless, chromosomes 6 and 9 were highlighted as the ones with fewer and more alterations, respectively, but the number of modifications is still inside the Tukey's Fences used on the outlier’s analysis.</p><p>The analysis considering the two separate individuals also supports chromosome 6 as the most relevant to distinguish the WF and WM individuals, and the result for the pooled sample may have a great contribution from the WF, which seems to be particularly conserved on chromosome 6.</p><p>The chromosome 13 also contributes to distinguish WM and WF plants in terms of SNPs and InDels. We need to emphasize caution in extrapolating this result to sex, as differences observed here are putatively individual-specific. Chromosome 2, consensually associated to sex, is not highlighted by any one of these analyses.</p><p>Analysis of the two plant chromosomes revealed that SNPs and Indels are spread across all of them. There are regions with a very low number of modifications, which coincide with regions poorly sequenced in the reference genome, therefore, the correct read mapping was not possible.</p><p>Most of the modifications identified are found in non-coding regions, where the impact is not directly assessed. However, Shabalina et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Shabalina, S. A., Spiridonov, N. A. &amp; Kashina, A. Sounds of silence: synonymous nucleotides as a key to biological regulation and complexity. Nucleic Acids Res. 41, 2073–2094 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d342e1716">36</a></sup> associated non-coding and synonymous SNPs to the gene expression, as these modifications may promote modifications at mRNA structure with consequences at stability and decay, or even by interfering in RNA editing. Similar trends of coding/non-coding and synonymous/non-synonymous were previously associated to wild populations, and according to Williamson et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Williamson, R. J. et al. Evidence for widespread positive and negative selection in coding and conserved noncoding regions of Capsella grandiflora. PLoS Genet. 10, e1004622 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d342e1720">37</a></sup> could be considered an evolutionary trait of the population instead of a mark associated to domestication efforts, essentially in non-domesticated species. However, coding/non-coding and synonymous/non-synonymous ratios were also proved to be associated to domestication, for example in bean<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Bitocchi, E. et al. High level of nonsynonymous changes in common bean suggests that selection under domestication increased functional diversity at target traits. Front. Plant Sci. 7, 2005 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d342e1724">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Schmutz, J. et al. A reference genome for common bean and genome-wide analysis of dual domestications. Nat. Genet. 46, 707–713 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d342e1727">39</a></sup> and tomato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Bhardwaj, A., Dhar, Y. V., Asif, M. H. &amp; Bag, S. K. In silico identification of SNP diversity in cultivated and wild tomato species: insight from molecular simulations. Sci. Rep. 6, 38715 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d342e1731">40</a></sup>. The modifications identified in this paper, were found through a comparison between wild type specimens and a domesticated variety, and so, the domestication impact may not be completely discarded. Furthermore, a recent publication used 261 SNP markers to evaluate the genetic differences between Portuguese varieties and Portuguese wild populations (including the populations referred herein)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Cunha, J. et al.
