Genetic relationships among portuguese cultivated and wild Vitis vinifera L. germplasm. Front. Plant Sci. 11, 127 (2020)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d342e1735">41</a></sup>, to establish kinship relations and gene flow between wild individuals and cultivar varieties, as already reported by other studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="D’Onofrio, C. Introgression among cultivated and wild grapevine in Tuscany. Front. Plant Sci. 11, 202 (2020)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d342e1740">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Cunha, J. et al. Genetic relationships among portuguese cultivated and wild Vitis vinifera L. germplasm. Front. Plant Sci. 11, 127 (2020)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d342e1743">41</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Ramos-Madrigal, J. et al. Palaeogenomic insights into the origins of French grapevine diversity. Nat. Plants 5, 595 (2019)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d342e1746">42</a></sup>. This kind of genetic flow between wild and domesticated varieties may contribute to dilute the domestication impact in grapevine due to a systematic recap of wild genes/alleles. Considering these findings any direct conclusion or association between the SNPs and their cause, should be drawn with caution. It should be noted that Cunha et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Cunha, J. et al. Genetic relationships among portuguese cultivated and wild Vitis vinifera L. germplasm. Front. Plant Sci. 11, 127 (2020)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d342e1750">41</a></sup> found no evidence of backcrossing between the domesticated and the wild genomes specifically used in this genome resequencing. Neither the evolutionary pressure on these two independent populations nor the effects of the reference genome domestication, were diluted by back-crosses, which means that both hypotheses could be simultaneously contributing to the observed differences.</p><p>One of the most unexpected result is the low Ti/Tv ratio (transitions over transversions) in exons, regardless the SNP impact on protein sequences (synonymous or non-synonymous). Ti/Tv ratios have been associated with the genomic context of the modification and in fact, we clearly observe an association between the genomic context and the ratio. However, the ratio we have obtained on exons (1.4–1.9) highly contrasts with the values reported by Wang et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Wang, J., Raskin, L., Samuels, D. C., Shyr, Y. &amp; Guo, Y. Genome measures used for quality control are dependent on gene function and ancestry. Bioinformatics 31, 318–323 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d342e1757">27</a></sup> (circa 2.8) but is in line with the results identified by Vondras et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Vondras, A. M. et al. The genomic diversification of grapevine clones. BMC Genom. 20, 972 (2019)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d342e1761">7</a></sup>. A lower ratio as we observed, is getting closer to the ratio that should be observed if SNPs were random (0.5 as there are two transitions and four transversions). Low values of Ti/Tv were also observed in <i>Oryza sativa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Li, G. et al. Genome-wide sequencing of 41 rice (Oryza sativa L.) mutated lines reveals diverse mutations induced by fast-neutron irradiation. Mol. Plant 9, 1078–1081 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d342e1767">43</a></sup> and <i>Brassica rapa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Park, S., Yu, H.-J., Mun, J.-H. &amp; Lee, S.-C. Genome-wide discovery of DNA polymorphism in Brassica rapa. Mol. Genet. Genom. MGG 283, 135–145 (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d342e1773">44</a></sup>. It is evident and intriguing that, in plants, the higher the impact of a SNP, the lower is the Ti/Tv obtained.</p><p>The highest percentage of regions affected by modifications, are intergenic followed by upstream regions (5 kbp before a gene start). It is interesting to observe that the percentage of occurrences on the upstream regions are almost twice as the ones observed for downstream (5 kbp after gene ends), essentially as upstream regions are usually associated with the gene promotor region. When analyzing the modifications in CDS regions, we find that the majority of the events have impact on protein (corresponding to non-synonymous modifications or frame shifts). The high impact observed, suggests an intentional evolutionary direction beyond simple mutation randomness. The percentage of modifications within the CDS region is generally low, but the few ones detected are modifications with an expected biological impact. These results support the suggestion by Ramos et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Ramos, M. J. N. et al. Flower development and sex specification in wild grapevine. BMC Genom. 15, 1095 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d342e1780">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Ramos, M. J. N. et al. Deep analysis of wild Vitis flower transcriptome reveals unexplored genome regions associated with sex specification. Plant Mol. Biol. 93, 151–170 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d342e1783">33</a></sup>, indicating that both subspecies (<i>sylvestris</i> and <i>vinifera</i>) are mostly similar, however, the few differences found lead to significant protein modification.</p><p>The fact that the two individuals used in this study share only half of the identified SNPs and InDels, suggests that these individuals are not parentally related. This idea is also supported by the heterozygous over non-reference homozygous SNPs whose ratio has been associated with lineages<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Wang, J., Raskin, L., Samuels, D. C., Shyr, Y. &amp; Guo, Y. Genome measures used for quality control are dependent on gene function and ancestry. Bioinformatics 31, 318–323 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d342e1796">27</a></sup>. We obtained distinct het/nonref-hom ratios for each individual for the same genomic regions. Unfortunately, other <i>Vitis</i> studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Mercenaro, L., Nieddu, G., Porceddu, A., Pezzotti, M. &amp; Camiolo, S. Sequence polymorphisms and structural variations among four grapevine (Vitis vinifera L.) cultivars representing sardinian agriculture. Front. Plant Sci. 8, 1279 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d342e1803">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Massonnet, M. et al. The genetic basis of sex determination in grapes. Nat. Commun. 11, 1–12 (2020)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d342e1806">19</a></sup> lacks this kind of detail.</p><p>When considering SNPs and InDels in common (between the two wild individuals), we need to be caution when extrapolating the results to the entire wild population, as we are observing the pattern of only two individuals (one male and one female individuals). Nevertheless, as they were phenotypically distinct individuals (one producing male flowers and the other female flowers), the common modifications may correspond to wild-type specific characteristics.</p><p>As both individuals produce distinct flower types, some of the genetic differences found between them may be associated to sex. Curiously, chromosome 2, where the <i>locus</i> associated to sex has been reported by different studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Vondras, A. M. et al. The genomic diversification of grapevine clones. BMC Genom. 20, 972 (2019)." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d342e1820">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Liang, Z. et al. Whole-genome resequencing of 472 Vitis accessions for grapevine diversity and demographic history analyses. Nat. Commun. 10, 1190 (2019)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d342e1820_1">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Zhou, Y. et al. The population genetics of structural variants in grapevine domestication. Nat. Plants 5, 965–979 (2019)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d342e1823">9</a></sup>, has a similar number of SNPs and InDels, when compared to other chromosomes. Nevertheless, we performed a more detailed analysis of the sex-associated <i>locus</i> in chromosome 2. From the 46 genes studied on this chromosome, only one (VIT_202s0154g00030) has identical alleles in both wild-type individuals, and other gene was recently associated with male sterility (<i>INP1</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Badouin, H. et al. The wild grape genome sequence provides insights into the transition from dioecy to hermaphroditism during grape domestication. bioRxiv 2020.01.07.897082 (2020). &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1101/2020.01.07.897082&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d342e1833">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Massonnet, M. et al. The genetic basis of sex determination in grapes. Nat. Commun. 11, 1–12 (2020)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d342e1836">19</a></sup>, which makes relevant the question of whether or not the other 44 genes could be associated with sex. At present, we are not able to answer this question and further studies should be carried to determine the impact of these 44 genes, using more individuals of each flower type, in order to better associate each one of these genes with its putative role in flower sex determination. Additionally, the two individuals used on this resequencing project had already been tested for <i>VviAPRT3</i> and <i>VviFSEX</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Zhou, Y. et al. The population genetics of structural variants in grapevine domestication. Nat. Plants 5, 965–979 (2019)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d342e1846">9</a></sup> and the pattern was the expected for male and female.</p><p>Regarding the discussion of SNPs relevance, some of them located in coding regions, we need to be careful, since RNA editing has already been described as present in the nuclear transcripts of plants like <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Qulsum, U., Azad, Md. T. A. &amp; Tsukahara, T. Analysis of tissue-specific RNA editing events of genes involved in RNA editing in Arabidopsis thaliana. J. Plant Biol. 62, 351–358 (2019)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d342e1855">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Meng, Y. et al. RNA editing of nuclear transcripts in Arabidopsis thaliana. BMC Genom. 11, S12 (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d342e1858">25</a></sup> and <i>Vitis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Ramos, M. J. N. et al. RNA editing in inflorescences of wild grapevine unveils association to sex and development. bioRxiv 2020.03.03.974626 (2020). &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1101/2020.03.03.974626&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d342e1864">26</a></sup>. In <i>Vitis</i>, the two genomes included in this work, were compared with previous transcriptomic data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Coito, J. L. et al. VviAPRT3 and VviFSEX: two genes involved in sex specification able to distinguish different flower types in Vitis. Front. Plant Sci. 8, 98 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d342e1871">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Badouin, H. et al. The wild grape genome sequence provides insights into the transition from dioecy to hermaphroditism during grape domestication. bioRxiv 2020.01.07.897082 (2020). &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1101/2020.01.07.897082&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d342e1874">18</a></sup>. We need to highlight that the studied individuals were the same.</p><p>Eventual differences between DNA and RNA (RDDs) need to be considered and tested before concluding that SNPs have impact in plants. Combining the genomes published in this work and the transcriptomes already made public<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Ramos, M. J. N. et al. Flower development and sex specification in wild grapevine. BMC Genom. 15, 1095 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d342e1881">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Ramos, M. J. N. et al. Deep analysis of wild Vitis flower transcriptome reveals unexplored genome regions associated with sex specification. Plant Mol. Biol. 93, 151–170 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d342e1884">33</a></sup>, we identified 14 genes in the sex-determining region whose transcripts show RNA editing, the majority of them exclusive of the female plant (8 out of 14). <i>VviYABBY</i> (VIT_202s0154g00070) is one of those genes and presents a two-variants RNA editing in the 3′UTR region, where edited (T-to-A) and non-edited mRNAs are simultaneously detected, at one of the tested flower developmental stages (D stage). Since the two forms are present, female flowers simultaneously exhibit messenger molecules with uracil (as a thymine is encoded by the genomes of female and male individuals) and other molecules with adenosine. A second mRNA two-variants RNA editing event was detected for 3′UTR of <i>VviYABBY</i> on male individual (WM) on developmental stages D and H. Which is also interesting, is the RNA editing pattern of <i>VviPLATZ</i> (VIT_202s0154g00150), as male and female inflorescence show different RNA editing events, without overlap, including events within the CDS regions.</p><p>Considering the relevance of the CRIBI database in <i>Vitis</i> research, we need to stress the urgency of a complete curation of the genome deposited in that database. An effort should be carried out to resolve the sequences deposited in the “unknown” and “X_random” chromosomes, as these sequences usually correspond to alleles of the genes present in the remaining 19 chromosomes.</p><p>Summing up, the work described in this article contributes to the identification of putative specific modifications of <i>sylvestris</i> when compared to <i>vinifera</i>. This paper clarifies questions raised on previous studies using the transcriptome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Ramos, M. J. N. et al. Flower development and sex specification in wild grapevine. BMC Genom. 15, 1095 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d342e1912">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Ramos, M. J. N. et al. Deep analysis of wild Vitis flower transcriptome reveals unexplored genome regions associated with sex specification. Plant Mol. Biol. 93, 151–170 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d342e1915">33</a></sup>, where different levels of gene expression were observed between <i>sylvestris</i> and <i>vinifera</i> transcriptomes. The identification of SNPs and InDels in the promotor region of the genes (5 kbp upstream of the gene) may explain the differential expression levels detected. In addition, the nucleotide differences revealed here, within coding regions, suggest that different alleles or gene duplications may have distinct importance in <i>sylvestris</i> and in <i>vinifera</i>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Materials and methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Materials and methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><p>Sampling and DNA extraction. Representatives from <i>V. v. sylvestris</i> (wild type) were selected from INIAV, Dois Portos (Lisbon district, Portugal, 39.041395, − 9.181956), where a wild type collection was established. One of the selected individuals exhibited female flowers (WF) and the other displayed male flowers (WM). Fresh leaves were collected from each selected individual. Leaves were grinded in liquid nitrogen and the DNA was extracted and purified with the DNeasy Plant Mini Kit (Qiagen), according to the manufacturer´s instructions. DNA quantity and quality was evaluated using a microplate reader Synergy HT (Biotek, Germany), using the software Gen5 (Biotek, Germany) and confirmed on a 1.7% (w/v) agarose gel.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">High-throughput sequencing and reference assembly</h3><p>Genome sequences were obtained individually from each individual using 1 µg of DNA, on a Genome Sequencer Illumina HiSeq 4000 technology (125 bp PE; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#Fig1">1</a>). A total of 539,473,738 reads were generated on both sequencings. Low quality reads (phred quality lower than 15) and reads without mate were removed. Additionally, low quality bases were removed from 3′ and 5′ ends. Minimum read size obtained after base trimming was 36. For the WF genome, 216,895,404 reads (91.6% of the total) were used on further analyses, while the WM genome was represented with 277,949,176 high quality reads (91.8%). Considering the <i>V. v. vinifera</i> cv. PN40024 reference genome available<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d342e1956">1</a></sup>, we estimated a genome size of about 486 Mbp, for which our predicted coverage ranges between 56X (WF) and 72X (WM). For general proposes, the results obtained from the different individuals were merged together, to better represent a wild- type genome.</p><p>The good-quality reads were mapped against the reference genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d342e1963">1</a></sup> using BWA (0.7.15)<sup>45</sup> with the default parameters. About 97% of the input reads mapped correctly to the reference genome (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#Tab1">1</a>).</p><p>Considering the artificial coverage created due to PCR amplification during library preparation, the reads were submitted to Picard (1.131; <a href="https://broadinstitute.github.io/picard/">https://broadinstitute.github.io/picard/</a>). The MarkDuplicates tool from Picard was used to detect duplicated reads whose origin is expected to be a single fragment of DNA. MarkDuplicates marks the reads as duplicates if multiple reads pairs maps in the same genomic location and with the same orientation. The presence of insertions and deletions, on the new obtained genomes, compared to the reference genome, promoted the occurrence of punctual misalignments. These misalignments were locally realigned with GATK (3.5)<sup>46,47</sup>. Taking into consideration the reported quality score, sequencing cycle and context, a final Base Quality Recalibration was performed with GATK, to improve the base quality score of the reads. About 92% of the mapped reads were kept after these three refinement steps. Whenever not explicitly indicated, default parameters were used.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Variant analyses</h3><p>SNP and InDel identification between the wild genomes and the reference genome, was performed with GATK’s Haplotype Caller<sup>46,47</sup> and annotation according to the gene context was achieved with snpEff (4.3; <a href="https://snpeff.sourceforge.net">https://snpeff.sourceforge.net</a>), according to PN40024 12X V2.1. The snpEff defaults for upstream and downstream were kept (5 kbp) and, when upstream and downstream regions overlap (adjacent genes), we considered the most relevant context: upstream instead of downstream. Variant quality was estimated with GATK’s Variant Annotator module<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="DePristo, M. A. et al. A framework for variation discovery and genotyping using next-generation DNA sequencing data. Nat. Genet. 43, 491–498 (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d342e2001">46</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="McKenna, A. et al. The genome analysis toolkit: a mapreduce framework for analyzing next-generation DNA sequencing data. Genome Res. 20, 1297–1303 (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d342e2004">47</a></sup> and false positives were filtered with GATK’s Variant Filtration module<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="DePristo, M. A. et al. A framework for variation discovery and genotyping using next-generation DNA sequencing data. Nat. Genet. 43, 491–498 (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d342e2008">46</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="McKenna, A. et al. The genome analysis toolkit: a mapreduce framework for analyzing next-generation DNA sequencing data. Genome Res. 20, 1297–1303 (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d342e2011">47</a></sup>. Options for GATK’s Variant Filtration module were: (1) LowCovFilter: ≤ 20; (2) QDFilter: &lt; 2.0; (3) MQFilter: &lt; <b> − </b>12.5; (4) FSFilter: &gt; 60.0 (&gt; 200.0 of InDels); (5) HaplotypeFilter: &gt; 13.0; (6) MQFilter: &lt; <b> − </b>12.5 and (7) ReadPosFilter: &lt; <b> − </b>8.0 (&lt; <b>− </b>20.0 for InDels). Analysis were performed on RStudio (1.0.143)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="RStudio Team. RStudio: Integrated Development Environment for R. (RStudio, Inc,, Boston, 2015)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d342e2028">48</a></sup>, running over R (3.4.3)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="R Core Team. R: A Language and Environment for Statistical Computing. (R Foundation for Statistical Computing, 2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d342e2032">49</a></sup>. Outliers were estimated based on Tukey's fences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Tukey, J. W. Exploratory Data Analysis (Addison-Wesley Pub, Boston, 1977)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d342e2036">50</a></sup>, considering k = 1.5. Venn Diagrams were obtained from calculate.overlap and draw.pairwise.venn functions (VennDiagram package, version 1.6.17<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Hanbo, C. VennDiagram: Generate High-Resolution Venn and Euler Plots. (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d342e2041">51</a></sup>). Heatmap analysis on SNPs and InDels density along chromosomes was performed with the R build-in heatmap function.</p><p>Transitions over transversion ratio (Ti/Tv) as well as heterozygous over non-reference homozygous SNPs (he/nonref-hom) were calculated for each genome with SNPs grouped by chromosome (discarding the “Un” and “X_random” virtual chromosomes). Calculations were performed with Python (3.7.4) running over Jupyter Notebook (6.0.1). Modules pandas (0.25.1) and matplotlib (3.1.1) were used to manipulate data and draw boxplots. Whenever not explicitly indicated, default parameters were used. Data from WF and WM was treated separately and pooled only for plotting and conclusions.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">De novo assembly</h3><p>A de novo assembly of the genomes was performed in order to obtain the genomic sequences of <i>loci</i> that were unclear on the reference genome. From the bulk of sequenced reads (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#Tab2">2</a>), an assessment was performed using FastqC software (v0.11.7). Considering the quality indicators, the reads were cleaned with BBDuk (v38.01) from BBMap package. A pipeline was set to remove adapters from both ends (ktrim = r and ktrim = lm; with options k = 23, tpe and tbo, using the provided adapters.fa as reference) and low quality reads (options qtrim = rl and trimq = 20). Reads were prepared for assembly using fq2fa function from IDBA package (1.1.3)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Peng, Y., Leung, H. C., Yiu, S.-M. &amp; Chin, F. Y. IDBA–a practical iterative de Bruijn graph de novo assembler. in Annual international conference on research in computational molecular biology 426–440 (Springer, 2010)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d342e2062">52</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Peng, Y., Leung, H. C., Yiu, S.-M. &amp; Chin, F. Y. IDBA-UD: a de novo assembler for single-cell and metagenomic sequencing data with highly uneven depth. Bioinformatics 28, 1420–1428 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d342e2065">53</a></sup>, with options –merge and –filter. Assembly was conducted with idba_ud. The contigs obtained with less than 1,000 bp were removed, as this represent low informative segments.</p><p>Contigs obtained on de novo assemblies were queried against the genes annotated on the sex region<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Picq, S. et al. A small XY chromosomal region explains sex determination in wild dioecious V. vinifera and the reversal to hermaphroditism in domesticated grapevines. BMC Plant Biol. 14, 229 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d342e2072">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Fechter, I. et al. Candidate genes within a 143 kb region of the flower sex locus in Vitis. Mol. Genet. Genom. 287, 247–259 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d342e2075">16</a></sup>, retrieved from the reference genome (hermaphrodite), on a blastn<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Altschul, S. F., Gish, W., Miller, W., Myers, E. W. &amp; Lipman, D. J. Basic local alignment search tool. J. Mol. Biol. 215, 403–410 (1990)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d342e2079">54</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Camacho, C. et al. BLAST+: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinf. 10, 421 (2009)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d342e2082">55</a></sup> run with e-value set to 1e − 10. The selected contigs were manually inspected. Although the usage of only two individuals, one of each sex, lacks the resolution to differentiate between sex-specific patterns and individual evolutionary mutations, this approach may provide a little insight into the genetic differences leading to different flower types.</p><p>The results of this assembly were validated by confirming the presence of contigs associated with <i>VviAPRT3</i> and <i>VviFSEX</i> as reported by Coito et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Coito, J. L. et al. VviAPRT3 and VviFSEX: two genes involved in sex specification able to distinguish different flower types in Vitis. Front. Plant Sci. 8, 98 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d342e2095">17</a></sup> and <i>VviINP1</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Badouin, H. et al. The wild grape genome sequence provides insights into the transition from dioecy to hermaphroditism during grape domestication. bioRxiv 2020.01.07.897082 (2020). &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1101/2020.01.07.897082&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d342e2101">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Massonnet, M. et al. The genetic basis of sex determination in grapes. Nat. Commun. 11, 1–12 (2020)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d342e2104">19</a></sup>, <i>VviPLATZ</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Badouin, H. et al. The wild grape genome sequence provides insights into the transition from dioecy to hermaphroditism during grape domestication. bioRxiv 2020.01.07.897082 (2020). &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1101/2020.01.07.897082&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d342e2111">18</a></sup> and <i>VviYABBY</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Massonnet, M. et al. The genetic basis of sex determination in grapes. Nat. Commun. 11, 1–12 (2020)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d342e2117">19</a></sup> and by testing the heterozygosity overlap between both assemblies (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#MOESM2">S1</a>). We manually inspected the region of chromosome 2 that has been associated with sex<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Picq, S. et al. A small XY chromosomal region explains sex determination in wild dioecious V. vinifera and the reversal to hermaphroditism in domesticated grapevines. BMC Plant Biol. 14, 229 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d342e2124">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Fechter, I. et al. Candidate genes within a 143 kb region of the flower sex locus in Vitis. Mol. Genet. Genom. 287, 247–259 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d342e2127">16</a></sup>. Whenever not explicitly indicated, default parameters were used.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">RNA editing</h3><p>The SNPs data obtained here was compared against the transcriptomic data obtained for the same individuals in 2014<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Ramos, M. J. N. et al. Flower development and sex specification in wild grapevine. BMC Genom. 15, 1095 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d342e2140">32</a></sup>. RNA was extracted from inflorescences WF and WM individuals at developmental stages B, D and H<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Baggiolini, M. Les stades repères dans le développement de la vigne et leur utilisation pratique (Station Féd, Essais Agric Lausanne, 1952)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d342e2144">56</a></sup>. Total RNA was extracted with Spectrum Total RNA kit (Sigma-Aldrich, Spain), according to the manufacturer’s instructions. cDNA libraries were obtained along standard Illumina pipeline, targeting poly-A tails. The obtained transcriptomic sequences were assembled against the reference genome (PN40024–12X), in transcriptome version 1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Vitulo, N. et al. A deep survey of alternative splicing in grape reveals changes in the splicing machinery related to tissue, stress condition and genotype. BMC Plant Biol. 14, 99 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d342e2148">2</a></sup>. SNPs were assessed between the transcriptomes and reference genome with CLC Genomics Workbench (Quiagen). Calls were discarded if: (1) the SNP call was within the first or last 60 bases of a linkage group sequence; (2) the reference genome base was ambiguous at the SNP position or within 60 bases of it; (3) the average copy number of the SNP flanking region was more than two; (4) the Illumina quality score of either allele was less than 20 and (5) the number of reads supporting either allele was less than two reads per accession. The initial list of variants was filtered using the Phred quality scores of the position and surrounding bases. To reduce false positives in variant calling, the minimum variant frequency was set to 15%. The minimum coverage required was 4 reads. Gene expression was measured using the number of Reads per Kilobase of exon model per Million mapped reads (RPKM).</p><p>Comparison between the RNA-seq data and the genomes sequenced was performed on R (3.4.3)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="R Core Team. R: A Language and Environment for Statistical Computing. (R Foundation for Statistical Computing, 2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d342e2155">49</a></sup>, running on a RStudio cluster (1.0.143)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="RStudio Team. RStudio: Integrated Development Environment for R. (RStudio, Inc,, Boston, 2015)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d342e2159">48</a></sup>. The comparison between the genome and the transcriptome was performed by assessing the exact genomic location of the SNPs, as both studies were mapped against the same reference genome. To guarantee low number of false positives, we have defined a set of conserved filters to increase confidence: (1) Each analysed site should have a minimum coverage of 5X (both in DNA-seq and RNA-seq, simultaneously); (2) the gene should be expressing in all samples (WF and WM individuals at inflorescence developmental stages B, D and H; RPKM ≥ 1); (3) events as deletions and inserts have been removed and (4) at least 10% of the reads covering the region should identify the nucleotide, otherwise that nucleotide was considered an error and discarded.</p><p>For sites where a SNP was identified only in DNA-seq or RNA-seq, a deeper analysis was performed, by assessing the information in the BAM files. In these situations, we evaluated if the lack of information was based on similarity between the genome/transcriptome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Ramos, M. J. N. et al. Flower development and sex specification in wild grapevine. BMC Genom. 15, 1095 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d342e2166">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Ramos, M. J. N. et al. Deep analysis of wild Vitis flower transcriptome reveals unexplored genome regions associated with sex specification. Plant Mol. Biol. 93, 151–170 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d342e2169">33</a></sup> sequenced, and the reference genome, or if the position was discarded by the filters defined. To the remaining sites, the homozygosity of the locus was computed considered the frequency of each allele obtained from the genome sequence described in this paper (freq ≥ 0.90, homozygous; freq &lt; 0.90, heterozygous). The comparison between the genome observed nucleotides and the transcriptome was achieved and, when the nucleotides observed in the transcriptome were not supported by the genomic information, we have considered RNA editing sites. We note that false RNA editing positives may be identified where the nucleotide observed in the transcriptome is present in less than 9% of the genome reads.</p><p>Interesting genes (reported in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#Fig6">6</a>) were manually inspected using the Integrated Genome Viewer (IGV) browser<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Robinson, J. T. et al. Integrative genomics viewer. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 24–26 (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d342e2179">57</a></sup>, loaded with the reference genome (PN40024–12X), the most recent gene annotation (V2.1) and the BAM files corresponding to the genome and transcriptomes sequencings, to validate the overall R analysis, with success.</p><p>As RNA editing was assessed based on cDNA libraries, we decided to refer to the DNA–RNA differences using the DNA and cDNA nomenclature (for example, using Thymine instead of Uracyl).</p><p>The R scripts developed will be available upon request, due to script’s authors stringencies.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">RNA editing validation by Sanger sequencing</h3><p>For individual WM and a secondary female individual (WF_2), RNA and DNA were extracted from the same inflorescence sample, at different developmental stages using Spectrum Plant Total RNA Kit (Sigma-Aldrich) and DNeasy Plant Mini Kit (Qiagen, Valencia, CA, USA), respectively, following manufacturer’s instructions. This approach allows to exclude putative somatic mutations that may occur along the development of the new branches. Total RNA was additionally treated with 30 units of DNase I (Qiagen). Nucleic acid concentration was measured using a microplate reader Synergy HT (Biotek, Germany), with the software Gen5 (Biotek, Germany). The cDNA was synthesized from 100 ng of total RNA, using oligo(dT)<sub>20</sub> and RevertAid Reverse Transcriptase (Thermo Fisher Scientific) in a 20 μL reaction volume, following the manufacturer’s instructions. cDNA concentration was measured as described above.</p><p>Two genes in chromosome 2, <i>VviYABBY</i> (VIT_202s0154g00070) and VIT_202s0033g01400 were manually inspected using IGV. Primers were designed for exons of <i>VviYABBY</i> and VIT_202s0033g01400 genes using Primer Premier5 (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#MOESM3">S2</a>). For <i>VviYABBY</i>, a 336 bp fragment (DNA and cDNA) was amplified and for VIT_202s0033g01400 a 829 bp fragment was obtain in DNA and 632 bp in cDNA amplification.</p><p>The DNA and the cDNA amplifications were performed through PCR in 25 μL total volume composed by 0.2 µg of DNA (or cDNA), PCR buffer (20 mM Tris–HCl [pH 8.4], 50 mM KCl), 1.5 mM of Mg<sub>2</sub>, 0.2 mM of dNTP mix, 0.4 μM of each forward and reverse primers, 10 U of Taq DNA polymerase and autoclaved MiliQ water. The applied program had 3 min for initial denaturation at 95 °C followed by 34 cycles of 45 s at 95 °C (denaturation), 45 s at 50 °C (annealing) for <i>VviYABBY</i> and 55 °C for VIT_202s0033g01400, and 90 s at 72 °C (extension), followed by a final extension for 5 min at 72 °C. PCR amplifications (1 μL) were inspected on an agarose gel (1%). The remaining product was purified using PureLink Quick PCR Purification kit (Thermo Fisher Scientific), according to the kit protocol. For each gene DNA and cDNA were Sanger sequenced in three technical replicates without cloning. The provided electropherograms were manually inspected.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Variant detection validation</h3><p>Four genes involved in flowering development were selected and specific primers were designed (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#MOESM3">S2</a>) using Primer Premier 5 (Premier Biosoft). Amplification reactions were performed in 25 µL composed by1 µg of DNA, PCR buffer (20 mM Tris–HCl [pH 8.4], 50 mM KCl), 1.5 mM of MgCl<sub>2</sub>, 0.2 mM of dNTP mix, 0.4 μM of each forward and reverse primers, 5 U of Taq DNA polymerase and autoclaved MiliQ water. The applied program had 4 min for initial denaturation at 94 °C; 30 cycles at 94 °C 45 s (denaturation), annealing at specific temperature (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-76012-6#MOESM3">S2</a>) 45 s (annealing) and 72 °C 90 s (extension); and a final extension at 72 °C for 4 min. PCR products were purified using PureLink (Invitrogen) and sequenced by Sanger without cloning.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>The raw data analysed in this study was submitted to the Sequence Read Archive (SRA) database (<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/">www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/</a>) under the number PRJNA549602. Scripts are available at the GitHub repository <a href="https://github.com/sex-group/wild-grapevine-genome">https://github.com/sex-group/wild-grapevine-genome</a>. The transcriptomes used in the RNA editing analysis are available at the Gene Expression Omnibus (GEO) database (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/geo/">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/geo/</a>) with the accession GSE56844.</p>
